    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The chromosome-scale genome reveals the evolution and diversification after the recent tetraploidization event in tea plant"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-05-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Tea is one of the most popular nonalcoholic beverages due to its characteristic secondary metabolites with numerous health benefits. Although two draft genomes of tea plant (Camellia sinensis) have been published recently, the lack of chromosome-scale assembly hampers the understanding of the fundamental genomic architecture of tea plant and potential improvement. Here, we performed a genome-wide chromosome conformation capture technique (Hi-C) to obtain a chromosome-scale assembly based on the draft genome of C. sinensis var. sinensis and successfully ordered 2984.7&#8201;Mb (94.7%) scaffolds into 15 chromosomes. The scaffold N50 of the improved genome was 218.1&#8201;Mb, ~157-fold higher than that of the draft genome. Collinearity comparison of genome sequences and two genetic maps validated the high contiguity and accuracy of the chromosome-scale assembly. We clarified that only one Camellia recent tetraploidization event (CRT, 58.9&#8211;61.7 million years ago (Mya)) occurred after the core-eudicot common hexaploidization event (146.6&#8211;152.7 Mya). Meanwhile, 9243 genes (28.6%) occurred in tandem duplication, and most of these expanded after the CRT event. These gene duplicates increased functionally divergent genes that play important roles in tea-specific biosynthesis or stress response. Sixty-four catechin- and caffeine-related quantitative trait loci (QTLs) were anchored to chromosome assembly. Of these, two catechin-related QTL hotspots were derived from the CRT event, which illustrated that polyploidy has played a dramatic role in the diversification of tea germplasms. The availability of a chromosome-scale genome of tea plant holds great promise for the understanding of genome evolution and the discovery of novel genes contributing to agronomically beneficial traits in future breeding programs."/>

    <meta name="description" content="Tea is one of the most popular nonalcoholic beverages due to its characteristic secondary metabolites with numerous health benefits. Although two draft genomes of tea plant (Camellia sinensis) have been published recently, the lack of chromosome-scale assembly hampers the understanding of the fundamental genomic architecture of tea plant and potential improvement. Here, we performed a genome-wide chromosome conformation capture technique (Hi-C) to obtain a chromosome-scale assembly based on the draft genome of C. sinensis var. sinensis and successfully ordered 2984.7&#8201;Mb (94.7%) scaffolds into 15 chromosomes. The scaffold N50 of the improved genome was 218.1&#8201;Mb, ~157-fold higher than that of the draft genome. Collinearity comparison of genome sequences and two genetic maps validated the high contiguity and accuracy of the chromosome-scale assembly. We clarified that only one Camellia recent tetraploidization event (CRT, 58.9&#8211;61.7 million years ago (Mya)) occurred after the core-eudicot common hexaploidization event (146.6&#8211;152.7 Mya). Meanwhile, 9243 genes (28.6%) occurred in tandem duplication, and most of these expanded after the CRT event. These gene duplicates increased functionally divergent genes that play important roles in tea-specific biosynthesis or stress response. Sixty-four catechin- and caffeine-related quantitative trait loci (QTLs) were anchored to chromosome assembly. Of these, two catechin-related QTL hotspots were derived from the CRT event, which illustrated that polyploidy has played a dramatic role in the diversification of tea germplasms. The availability of a chromosome-scale genome of tea plant holds great promise for the understanding of genome evolution and the discovery of novel genes contributing to agronomically beneficial traits in future breeding programs."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Tea is one of the most popular nonalcoholic beverages due to its characteristic secondary metabolites with numerous health benefits. Although two draft genomes of tea plant (<i>Camellia sinensis</i>) have been published recently, the lack of chromosome-scale assembly hampers the understanding of the fundamental genomic architecture of tea plant and potential improvement. Here, we performed a genome-wide chromosome conformation capture technique (Hi-C) to obtain a chromosome-scale assembly based on the draft genome of <i>C. sinensis</i> var. <i>sinensis</i> and successfully ordered 2984.7 Mb (94.7%) scaffolds into 15 chromosomes. The scaffold N50 of the improved genome was 218.1 Mb, ~157-fold higher than that of the draft genome. Collinearity comparison of genome sequences and two genetic maps validated the high contiguity and accuracy of the chromosome-scale assembly. We clarified that only one <i>Camellia</i> recent tetraploidization event (CRT, 58.9–61.7 million years ago (Mya)) occurred after the core-eudicot common hexaploidization event (146.6–152.7 Mya). Meanwhile, 9243 genes (28.6%) occurred in tandem duplication, and most of these expanded after the CRT event. These gene duplicates increased functionally divergent genes that play important roles in tea-specific biosynthesis or stress response. Sixty-four catechin- and caffeine-related quantitative trait loci (QTLs) were anchored to chromosome assembly. Of these, two catechin-related QTL hotspots were derived from the CRT event, which illustrated that polyploidy has played a dramatic role in the diversification of tea germplasms. The availability of a chromosome-scale genome of tea plant holds great promise for the understanding of genome evolution and the discovery of novel genes contributing to agronomically beneficial traits in future breeding programs.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Tea, one of the most popular nonalcoholic beverages in the world, provides characteristic secondary metabolites, such as catechins, theanine, and caffeine that have numerous health benefits for humans<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kanwar, J. et al. Recent advances on tea polyphenols. Front. Biosci. 4, 111–131 (2012)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d4353e439">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Pervin, M. et al. Beneficial effects of green tea catechins on neurodegenerative diseases. Molecules 23, 1297 (2018)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4353e439_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Mancini, E. et al. Green tea effects on cognition, mood and human brain function: a systematic review. Phytomedicine 34, 26–37 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d4353e442">3</a></sup>. The tea plant (<i>Camellia sinensis</i> (L.) O. Kuntze) originated in southwest China and has expanded worldwide to &gt;50 countries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen, L., Apostolides, Z. &amp; Chen, Z. M. Global Tea Breeding: Achievements, Challenges and Perspectives (University Press-Springer, Hangzhou, Zhejiang; 2012)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d4353e449">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen, L., Yao, M. Z., Wang, X. C. &amp; Yang, Y. J. Tea genetic resources in China. Int. J. Tea Sci. 8(2), 55–64 (2012)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d4353e449_1">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Yao, M. Z., Ma, C. L., Qiao, T. T., Jin, J. Q. &amp; Chen, L. Diversity distribution and population structure of tea germplasms in China revealed by EST-SSR markers. Tree Genet. Genomes 8, 205–220 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4353e452">6</a></sup>. By 2018, the worldwide cultivated area and production of tea have increased to 4.9 million hectares and 5.9 million tons, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="International Tea Committee. Annual Bulletin of Statistics (London, 2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d4353e456">7</a></sup>. Tea has created economic benefits and vast employment opportunities, especially in some Asian and African countries, such as China, India, Sri Lanka, and Kenya. In addition, due to distinctive sets of secondary metabolites (such as various catechins, caffeine, and theanine), tea has been widely applied in expounding the molecular mechanisms regulating catechins and theanine biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Rani, A., Singh, K., Sood, P., Kumar, S. &amp; Ahuja, P. S. p-Coumarate:CoA ligase as a key gene in the yield of catechins in tea [Camellia sinensis (L.) O. Kuntze]. Funct. Integr. Genomics 9, 271–275 (2009)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d4353e460">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Singh, K. et al. An early gene of the flavonoid pathway, flavanone 3-hydroxylase, exhibits a positive relationship with the concentration of catechins in tea (Camellia sinensis). Tree Physiol. 28, 1349–1356 (2008)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d4353e460_1">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Rani, A., Singh, K., Ahuja, P. S. &amp; Kumar, S. Molecular regulation of catechins biosynthesis in tea [Camellia sinensis (L.) O. Kuntze]. Gene 495, 205–210 (2012)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4353e460_2">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Pang, Y. et al. Functional characterization of proanthocyanidin pathway enzymes from tea and their application for metabolic engineering. Plant Physiol. 161, 1103–1116 (2013)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d4353e460_3">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Deng, W. W., Ogita, S. &amp; Ashihara, H. Ethylamine content and theanine biosynthesis in different organs of Camellia sinensis seedlings. Z. Naturforsch. C. J. Biosci. 64, 387–390 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d4353e463">12</a></sup>. However, the lack of a high-quality genome sequence has become the main hindrance to gaining insights into secondary metabolite biosynthesis and fully understanding the evolution of tea plant. High levels of heterozygosity and repetitiveness pose a challenge to genome assembly in tea plant. Recently, two draft genomes of tea plant have been published by Illumina next-generation sequencing technology, but these genomes remain highly fragmented (scaffold N50: 449.5 kb in <i>C. sinensis</i> var. <i>assamica</i> genome and 1.4 Mb in <i>C. sinensis</i> var. <i>sinensis</i> (CSS) genome) and need improved completion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wei, C. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E4151–E4158 (2018)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d4353e480">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Xia, E. H. et al. The tea tree genome provides insights into tea flavor and independent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 10, 866–877 (2017)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4353e480_1">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Xia, E. et al. The tea plant reference genome and improved gene annotation using long-read and paired-end sequencing data. Sci. Data 6, 122 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d4353e483">15</a></sup>.</p><p>Chromosome-scale genome assembly is essential for genome-wide association study (GWAS) and the identification of quantitative trait loci (QTLs) governing important agronomic traits to facilitate gene cloning<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Philippe, R. et al. A high density physical map of chromosome 1BL supports evolutionary studies, map-based cloning and sequencing in wheat. Genome Biol. 14, R64 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d4353e490">16</a></sup>. A high-quality reference genome also accelerates genome evolution involving ancient whole-genome duplications (WGDs), segmental duplications, tandem duplications, structural evolution, etc. The traditional BAC-by-BAC approach, integrating genetic and physical maps, has been used to produce high-quality assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d4353e494">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112–1115 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d4353e497">18</a></sup>. However, this approach remains prohibitively expensive and laborious, which are bottlenecks for its widespread application to genome assembly. A genetic linkage map is another approach that assigns contigs or scaffolds to chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Prochnik, S. et al. The cassava genome: current progress, future directions. Tropical Plant Biol. 5, 88–94 (2012)." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d4353e501">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jia, J. et al. Aegilops tauschii draft genome sequence reveals a gene repertoire for wheat adaptation. Nature 496, 91–95 (2013)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d4353e501_1">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Wang, S. et al. Sequence-based ultra-dense genetic and physical maps reveal structural variations of allopolyploid cotton genomes. Genome Biol. 16, 108 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d4353e504">21</a></sup>. A high-density SLAF-seq (SNP and SSR) genetic map of tea plant has been used to anchor scaffolds to chromosomes in the draft genome of CSS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wei, C. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E4151–E4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d4353e508">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Ma, J. Q. et al. Large-scale SNP discovery and genotyping for constructing a high-density genetic map of tea plant using Specific-Locus Amplified Fragment Sequencing (SLAF-seq). PLoS ONE 10, e0128798 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d4353e511">22</a></sup>. However, it is often difficult to generate a high-quality genome sequence, especially in centromeric regions, depending on the density of molecular markers, mapping population, and recombination events. Recently, Hi-C (high-throughput/resolution chromosome conformation capture), an effective and efficient approach, has been developed to guide genome assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kaplan, N. &amp; Dekker, J. High-throughput genome scaffolding from in vivo DNA interaction frequency. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1143–1147 (2013)." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d4353e515">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119–1125 (2013)." href="#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d4353e515_1">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Dekker, J., Marti-Renom, M. A. &amp; Mirny, L. A. Exploring the three-dimensional organization of genomes: interpreting chromatin interaction data. Nat. Rev. Genet. 14, 390–403 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d4353e518">25</a></sup>. Due to the highly folded structures of chromosomes in a cell, sequences that are spatially proximal in three-dimensional (3D) space can be far apart along the linear chromosome. Hi-C detects spatially proximal DNA interactions by a proximity ligation method combined with Illumina sequencing. The frequency of contact between paired-end reads of loci can infer the distance of two DNA fragments, and further anchor and orient scaffolds. Hi-C has become a powerful approach for generating high-quality genome assemblies and has been widely performed in the genome assembly of animals and plants, such as humans<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119–1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d4353e523">24</a></sup>, <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Xie, T. et al. De novo plant genome assembly based on chromatin interactions: a case study of Arabidopsis thaliana. Mol. Plant 8, 489–492 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d4353e529">26</a></sup>, and black raspberry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Jibran, R. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of the black raspberry (Rubus occidentalis L.) genome based on chromatin interaction data. Hortic. Res. 5, 8 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d4353e533">27</a></sup>.</p><p>WGD, also known as polyploidy, has been widely detected in plants and is considered an important evolutionary force in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Qiao, X. et al. Gene duplication and evolution in recurring polyploidization-diploidization cycles in plants. Genome Biol. 20, 38 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d4353e540">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Ancestral polyploidy in seed plants and angiosperms. Nature 473, 97–100 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d4353e543">29</a></sup>. The WGD events are followed by chromosomal rearrangement, gene loss of most duplicates, and duplicate gene expression bias, causing a dramatic increase in species richness in angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Soltis, D. E. et al. Polyploidy and angiosperm diversification. Am. J. Bot. 96, 336–348 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d4353e547">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Wendel, J. F., Jackson, S. A., Meyers, B. C. &amp; Wing, R. A. Evolution of plant genome architecture. Genome Biol. 17, 37 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d4353e550">31</a></sup>. After the divergence of eudicots and monocots, a hexaploidization event (γ event) shared by all core eudicots occurred 140 million years ago (Mya)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Moore, M. J., Bell, C. D., Soltis, P. S. &amp; Soltis, D. E. Using plastid genome-scale data to resolve enigmatic relationships among basal angiosperms. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 104, 19363–19368 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d4353e554">32</a></sup>. Because the <i>Vitis vinifera</i> genome had no additional WGD event except the hexaploidization event and preserved the ancestral eudicot chromosome structure, it has been widely used as a reference genome for the study of evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature 492, 423–427 (2012)." href="#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d4353e561">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang, J. et al. An overlooked paleotetraploidization in Cucurbitaceae. Mol. Biol. Evol. 35, 16–26 (2018)." href="#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d4353e561_1">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Wang, J. P. et al. Two likely auto-tetrapl oidization events shaped kiwi fruit genome and contributed to establishment of the Actinidiaceae family. iScience 7, 230–240 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d4353e564">35</a></sup>. <i>Actinidia chinensis</i>, the closest genus to <i>Camellia</i> in phylogenetic trees, showed two additional tetraploidization events after the hexaploidization event, namely, the <i>Actinidia</i> recent tetraploidization at ~18–20 Mya and the <i>Actinidia</i> ancient tetraploidization at ~50–57 Mya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Wang, J. P. et al. Two likely auto-tetrapl oidization events shaped kiwi fruit genome and contributed to establishment of the Actinidiaceae family. iScience 7, 230–240 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d4353e581">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Wu, H. et al. A high-quality Actinidia chinensis (kiwifruit) genome. Horticulture Res. 6, 117 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d4353e584">36</a></sup>. With the completion of the draft genome sequences of <i>C. sinensis</i>, two opinions regarding WGDs in the <i>C. sinensis</i> genome appeared: that two additional rounds of WGD events occurred in <i>C. sinensis</i> after the hexaploidization event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wei, C. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E4151–E4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d4353e598">13</a></sup> and that only one additional WGD event occurred in <i>C. sinensis</i> independent with tetraploidization in <i>A. chinensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Xia, E. H. et al. The tea tree genome provides insights into tea flavor and independent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 10, 866–877 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4353e607">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Wu, H. et al. A high-quality Actinidia chinensis (kiwifruit) genome. Horticulture Res. 6, 117 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d4353e610">36</a></sup>. Tandem duplicates that arise by unequal crossing over are closely adjacent identical genes in the same chromosome and are prevalent in eukaryotes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Freeling, M. Bias in plant gene content following different sorts of duplication: tandem, whole-genome, segmental, or by transposition. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 60, 433–453 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d4353e615">37</a></sup>. Tandem duplicates usually tend to share common functions owing to co-regulated elements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Li, X. et al. Genome-wide analysis of basic/helix-loop-helix transcription factor family in rice and Arabidopsis. Plant Physiol. 141, 1167–1184 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d4353e619">38</a></sup>. However, it has been revealed that some tandem duplicates have generated novel functions by acquiring novel transcription patterns<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Fan, C., Chen, Y. &amp; Long, M. Recurrent tandem gene duplication gave rise to functionally divergent genes in Drosophila. Mol. Biol. Evol. 25, 1451–1458 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d4353e623">39</a></sup>. Moreover, tandem duplicates play an important role in adaptive evolution to rapidly changing environments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Hanada, K., Zou, C., Lehti-Shiu, M. D., Shinozaki, K. &amp; Shiu, S. H. Importance of lineage-specific expansion of plant tandem duplicates in the adaptive response to environmental stimuli. Plant Physiol. 148, 993–1003 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d4353e627">40</a></sup>.</p><p>In this study, Hi-C analysis of CSS ‘Shuchazao’ was performed to improve the draft genome sequence of CSS, generating a chromosome-scale assembly for tea plant. Based on the chromosome-scale genome, WGD and tandem duplication were identified to investigate recursive polyploidizations and diversification of duplicate genes. Furthermore, the published QTLs related to the catechins and caffeine content in tea were integrated into the chromosome-scale genome to facilitate the identification of the effective genes. These results improved our understanding of the evolution and diversification of duplicated genes in tea plant, laying a substantial foundation for the discovery of novel genes contributing to agronomically beneficial traits in future breeding programs.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Chromosome-scale assembly of CSS</h3><p>To obtain a chromosome-scale assembly of tea plant, a total of 1,126,108,661 Hi-C read pairs of CSS ‘Shuchazao’ (337.8 Gb, ~113-fold genome coverage) were generated and mapped to the draft genome of CSS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wei, C. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E4151–E4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d4353e646">13</a></sup>. After the removal of erroneous mappings and PCR duplicates, the remaining 507,043,204 read pairs (45.0% of total reads) were used to construct Hi-C linking information and a Hi-C scaffolding pipeline (misjoin correction, ordering, and orientation). Finally, the chromosome-scale assembly of the CSS V1.2 genome spanning 3.2 Gb of genome sequence was generated. In this study, the 2984.7 Mb genome sequence was clustered into 15 superscaffolds, accounting for 94.7% of the total genome size. The scaffold N50 of the CSS V1.2 genome was 218.1 Mb, ~157-fold higher than that of the draft genome (1.4 Mb, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#MOESM1">1</a>). The Hi-C heatmap showed that 15 superscaffolds in the CSS V1.2 genome could be distinguished and perfectly represented 15 chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#Fig1">1a</a>). The chromosome names from chr1 to chr15 were defined by the previous genetic map from LG01 to LG15, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Ma, J. Q. et al. Large-scale SNP discovery and genotyping for constructing a high-density genetic map of tea plant using Specific-Locus Amplified Fragment Sequencing (SLAF-seq). PLoS ONE 10, e0128798 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d4353e656">22</a></sup>. Of the 15 chromosomes, the longest and shortest assembled pseudomolecules were chr3 and chr15 at 264,061,170 and 139,796,614 bp, respectively, and the average length was 198,979,785 bp (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#Fig1">1b</a>). To facilitate identification of functional genes, 32,311 protein-coding genes (95.2% of annotated genes in the draft genome) were anchored to the chromosomes of the CSS V1.2 genome. The number of genes on each chromosome varied significantly from 1467 to 3013 (mean, 2154) and showed that the gene number was correlated to chromosome size (<i>r</i><sup>2</sup> = 0.91, Pearson correlation coefficient). The size of transposable elements (TEs) had a higher coefficient of association (<i>r</i><sup>2</sup> = 0.99) than the number of genes. However, the proportion of TEs in each chromosome remained relatively constant, from 55.5% to 59.5% (mean, 57.4%, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Hi-C heatmap based on the chromosome-scale assembly of the CSS V1.2 genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Hi-C heatmap based on the chromosome-scale assembly of the CSS V1.2 genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0288-2/MediaObjects/41438_2020_288_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0288-2/MediaObjects/41438_2020_288_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="487" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> The heatmap represents the contact matrices generated by aligning the Hi-C data to the chromosome-scale assembly of the CSS V1.2 genome. <b>b</b> The length statistics of each chromosome of the CSS V1.2 genome resulting from the Juicer and 3D-DNA pipelines</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To assess the improved genome, 6042 and 2380 available markers from the SLAF-seq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Ma, J. Q. et al. Large-scale SNP discovery and genotyping for constructing a high-density genetic map of tea plant using Specific-Locus Amplified Fragment Sequencing (SLAF-seq). PLoS ONE 10, e0128798 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d4353e692">22</a></sup> and 2b-RAD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Xu, L. Y. et al. High-density SNP linkage map construction and QTL mapping for flavonoid-related traits in a tea plant (Camellia sinensis) using 2b-RAD sequencing. BMC Genomics 19, 955 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d4353e696">41</a></sup> genetic maps were assigned to the CSS V1.2 genome. After filtering multimapped sequences, 2035 and 1828 unambiguous alignments in the SLAF-seq and 2b-RAD genetic maps were retained, and 96.9% and 83.1% of these were correctly identified in the CSS V1.2 genome, respectively, in the sense that these markers appeared on the same chromosome in the genetic map and the CSS V1.2 genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#MOESM2">3</a>). Collinearity comparison between the physical map and the genetic map validated the high contiguity and accuracy of the CSS V1.2 genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#Fig2">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Comparison of the chromosome-scale assembly of the CSS V1.2 genome and genetic maps."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Comparison of the chromosome-scale assembly of the CSS V1.2 genome and genetic maps.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0288-2/MediaObjects/41438_2020_288_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0288-2/MediaObjects/41438_2020_288_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="324" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> The <i>y</i>-axis represents the genetic positions of the SLAF-seq genetic map from the F1 population of CSS ‘Yingshuang’ and <i>C. sinensis</i> var. <i>pubilimba</i> ‘Beiyue Danzhu’. <b>b</b> The <i>y</i>-axis represents the genetic positions of the 2b-RAD genetic map from the F1 population of CSS ‘Longjing 43’ and CSS ‘Baihaozao’. The <i>x</i>-axis represents the physical positions of the chromosome-scale assembly of the CSS V1.2 genome</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Whole-genome duplication</h3><p>Two opinions, namely, that one or two additional WGD events in <i>C. sinensis</i> occurred after the hexaploidization event (γ event) shared by the eudicot genome, have been reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wei, C. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E4151–E4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d4353e747">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Xia, E. H. et al. The tea tree genome provides insights into tea flavor and independent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 10, 866–877 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4353e750">14</a></sup>. To clarify the question of how many rounds of WGD events occurred in <i>C. sinensis</i>, the sequence divergence of homologous genes and gene dotplots were used to infer ancient polyploidization events. First, self-comparison of the <i>C. sinensis</i> genome was carried out, and 254 homologous blocks containing 4748 gene pairs were identified in the intragenomic gene collinearity of <i>C. sinensis</i>. The synonymous nucleotide substitutions (<i>K</i>s) of these homologous gene pairs were calculated. These homologous blocks resulted from multiple polyploidization events, so we classified them by the median <i>K</i>s of each homologous block based on the phenomenon that <i>K</i>s of homologous genes produced by one polyploidization event were usually similar. The histogram plot with the median <i>K</i>s of homologous blocks showed two clearly distinguishable peaks (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#MOESM1">1</a>), suggesting that two ancient polyploidization events exist in <i>C. sinensis</i>. The homologous blocks with relatively high <i>K</i>s values might have resulted from the core-eudicot common hexaploidization event (ECH), while other blocks (<i>K</i>s ≤ 0.7) might have been generated by the <i>Camellia</i> recent WGD event. The homologous gene dotplot within the <i>C. sinensis</i> genome integrated with the <i>K</i>s information further clarified that one <i>Camellia</i> recent tetraploidization event (CRT) occurred after the ECH event (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#MOESM1">2</a>). These blocks were classified into 177 ECH-related and 77 CRT-related homologous blocks, containing 2152 (45.3%, 12.2 gene pairs per block) and 2596 (54.7%, 33.7) gene pairs, respectively, revealing that CRT has a more highly conserved synteny than ECH in <i>C. sinensis</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#MOESM1">4</a>). To estimate the occurrence times of two polyploidization events, curve fitting of the <i>K</i>s distribution of homologous genes resulting from the ECH and CRT events was performed. The peaks of the two polyploidization events were at 0.4 and 1.0, suggesting that the CRT and ECH events occurred at 58.9–61.7 and 146.6–152.7 Mya based on a neutral substitution rate of 3.39 × 10<sup>−936</sup>, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#Fig3">3a</a>). Although the CRT event occurred near the divergence time of <i>C. sinensis</i> and <i>A. chinensis</i> from their common ancestor (61.2–65.3 Mya), a previous report indicates that the tetraploidization event was a lineage-specific WGD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Xia, E. H. et al. The tea tree genome provides insights into tea flavor and independent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 10, 866–877 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4353e826">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Wu, H. et al. A high-quality Actinidia chinensis (kiwifruit) genome. Horticulture Res. 6, 117 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d4353e829">36</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#Fig3">3b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Dating of two polyploidization events of the C. sinensis genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Dating of two polyploidization events of the <i>C. sinensis</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0288-2/MediaObjects/41438_2020_288_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0288-2/MediaObjects/41438_2020_288_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="419" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> <i>K</i>s distribution between collinear genes within the <i>C. sinensis</i> genome or among genomes. CRT <i>Camellia</i> recent tetraploidization, ECH core-eudicot common hexaploidization, ART <i>Actinidia</i> recent tetraploidization, AAT <i>Actinidia</i> ancient tetraploidization. <b>b</b> Phylogenetic tree of <i>C. sinensis</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i>, and <i>A. chinensis</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To further verify two polyploidization events (ECH and CRT) in <i>C. sinensis</i>, the CSS V1.2 genome was compared with <i>V. vinifera</i>, known to be the closest relative to the eudicot ancestor structured. Based on the all-vs-all blastp analysis, 714 homologous blocks containing 14,831 gene pairs between <i>C. sinensis</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> were identified (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#MOESM1">5</a>). Using <i>V. vinifera</i> as the reference genome, for almost every <i>V. vinifera</i> chromosome, six collinear regions can be identified in the <i>C. sinensis</i> genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#Fig4">4</a>). Among these, the best and second best-matched <i>V. vinifera</i> regions resulted from the CRT event and ECH event, respectively. Thus, this 1-to-2 relationship between <i>V. vinifera</i> and <i>C. sinensis</i> genomic regions inferred from the best-matching <i>V. vinifera</i> regions was a clear indication of a tetraploidization event in the <i>C. sinensis</i> genome after the split with <i>V. vinifera</i>. In addition, the following <i>C. sinensis</i> chromosome to <i>V. vinifera</i> chromosome correspondences were established (c for <i>C. sinensis</i> and v for <i>V. vinifera</i> as chromosome nomenclature): c1/v18-v8-v3-v6, c2/v1-v11-v15-v14-v3, c3/v13-v16-v14-v4-v7, c4/v5-v19-v12, c5/v10, c6/v3-v15-v14-v13-v12, c7/v8-v6-v7-v3-v12, c8/v11-v9-v17-v14-v7-v4, c9/v12-v19-v13-v16, c10/v5-v18, c11/v6-v7, c12/v8-v4-v1, c13/v2-v4-v11, c14/v1-v2-v4, c15/v9-v17. Among these, c5 was fused with its chromosomal segment duplications corresponding to v10. However, the fission and fusion of at least five chromosomes resulted in the longest chromosome c3.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Homologous dotplot between C. sinensis and V. vinifera genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Homologous dotplot between <i>C. sinensis</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0288-2/MediaObjects/41438_2020_288_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0288-2/MediaObjects/41438_2020_288_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="683" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>The red, blue, and gray dots represent the best, secondary, and other matched homologous gene pairs resulting from the output of Blast software. The 19 chromosomes of the <i>V. vinifera</i> genome are colored by the seven eudicot ancestral chromosomes. The orthologous regions were identified and marked by squares</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Polyploidization events increased the complexity of the <i>C. sinensis</i> genome, including genome size, gene content, and gene expression. For example, <i>C. sinensis</i> chr5 mainly contained two intrachromosomal segmental duplications, suggesting that it fused with its homolog after the recent tetraploidization event, corresponding to chr10 in <i>V. vinifera</i>. Although 250 paralogous gene pairs exhibited substantially conserved synteny between chr5a (chr5:21.3–86.1 Mb) and chr5b (chr5:89.9–218.1 Mb), these genes accounted for only 25.4% and 35.6% of all genes in chr5a and chr5b, respectively, and most genes exhibited divergence between the two duplicates. Moreover, the segmental length of chr5b was twice that of chr5a, resulting from variation in the number of TEs during genome evolution and diversification (78.3 Mb TEs in chr5b and 36.4 Mb in chr5a, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#MOESM1">3a</a>). Among these retained genes both in chr5a and chr5b, expression divergence between duplicate genes was widespread during evolution, and 50.5–57.8% of duplicate genes had differential expression in one of the eight tissues (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#MOESM1">3b</a>). The duplicate genes of asymmetrical evolution in sequence, structure, and expression may be important for the evolution of the <i>C. sinensis</i> genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Tandem gene duplication</h3><p>Tandem duplication was also detected by BLASTP (<i>e</i>-value &lt; 1e−20), with a maximum of five intervening genes. A total of 3262 tandem expanded regions were detected in the <i>C. sinensis</i> genome and were distributed unevenly on 15 chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#Fig5">5a</a>). The tandem expanded regions contained 9243 genes (28.6% of all genes on the chromosomes), which was higher than the numbers in <i>A. chinensis</i> (3111, 10.1%) and <i>V. vinifera</i> (5088, 21.5%, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#MOESM1">6</a>). The most tandem duplicates in the <i>C. sinensis</i> genome were the result of a steady and unusually high rate of tandem duplicate gain after the recent tetraploidization event (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#MOESM1">4</a>). The tandem genes had significantly lower expression levels than the nontandem genes in each of eight tissues (apical bud, young leaf, mature leaf, old leaf, young stem, root, flower, and fruit), and 26.5% of tandem genes were not expressed (FPKM &lt; 1) in all tissues (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#Fig5">5c</a>). These results revealed that the recently expanded or lineage-specific genes had lower expression, consistent with a previous report that gene expression was positively correlated with the age of gene occurrence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Stein, J. C. et al. Genomes of 13 domesticated and wild rice relatives highlight genetic conservation, turnover and innovation across the genus Oryza. Nat. Genet. 50, 285–296 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d4353e1018">42</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Genome-wide analysis of tandem gene duplication in the C. sinensis genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Genome-wide analysis of tandem gene duplication in the <i>C. sinensis genome</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0288-2/MediaObjects/41438_2020_288_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0288-2/MediaObjects/41438_2020_288_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="595" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b> <i>C. sinensis</i> genome topography and synteny. I represents the duplicated gene blocks within <i>C. sinensis</i> that were produced by ECH (green) and CRT (orange) events. II represents the frequency distribution of tandem genes in 1-Mb intervals across the 15 chromosomes, MAX = 22. III represents gene density ranging from 0 to 37 genes per Mb. <b>b</b> Identification of tandemly duplicated NBS-encoding genes. <b>c</b> Comparison of gene expression in eight tissues between tandem and nontandem NBS-encoding genes. <b>d</b> Comparison of the highly expressed apical genes between tandem and nontandem genes among the enriched genes involved in phenylpropanoid biosynthesis and flavonoid biosynthesis. <b>e</b> Phylogenetic tree and gene expression in eight tissues of six F3ʹ5ʹH genes belonging to two clusters</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Furthermore, KEGG (Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes) enrichment analysis of tandem duplication indicated that most of these genes were related to plant–pathogen interaction (ko04626), MAPK signaling pathway (ko04016), phenylpropanoid biosynthesis (ko00940), flavonoid biosynthesis (ko00941), anthocyanin biosynthesis (ko00942), isoflavonoid biosynthesis (ko00943), etc. (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#MOESM2">7</a>). The tandem duplication retained in a lineage-specific fashion participated in abiotic and biotic stress tolerance, suggesting that tandem duplication played an important role in adaptive evolution to rapidly changing environments and/or interaction with pathogens<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Hanada, K., Zou, C., Lehti-Shiu, M. D., Shinozaki, K. &amp; Shiu, S. H. Importance of lineage-specific expansion of plant tandem duplicates in the adaptive response to environmental stimuli. Plant Physiol. 148, 993–1003 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d4353e1062">40</a></sup>. More than 72.5% of the nucleotide binding site (NBS) genes, an important resistance (<i>R</i>) gene family, occurred in tandem duplication. These NBS genes could be divided into six classes according to their conserved domains: N, NL, CNL, CN, TN, and RN. The majority were N type and contained only the NB-ARC domain (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#Fig5">5b</a>). Expression analysis in eight tissues, a measurement of the activity and function of genes, showed that NBS genes were highly or preferentially expressed in the root (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#Fig5">5c</a>), resulting from the greater pressure on the root than the other tissues<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Fu, Y. et al. NBS-encoding genes in Brassica napus evolved rapidly after allopolyploidization and co-localize with known disease resistance loci. Front. Plant Sci. 10, 26 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d4353e1076">43</a></sup>. Among these NBS genes, the tandem duplication did not show lower expression levels, which was inconsistent with the overall result. These highly expressed tandem duplicates of NBS genes might increase <i>C. sinensis</i> resistance to dynamic environments and pathogens. Catechin, a type of flavonoid (flavan-3-ol), is synthesized through phenylpropanoid and flavonoid biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Winkel-Shirley, B. Flavonoid biosynthesis. A colorful model for genetics, biochemistry, cell biology, and biotechnology. Plant Physiol. 126, 485–493 (2001)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d4353e1083">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Guo, F., Guo, Y., Wang, P., Wang, Y. &amp; Ni, D. Transcriptional profiling of catechins biosynthesis genes during tea plant leaf development. Planta 246, 1139–1152 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d4353e1086">45</a></sup>. KEGG analysis indicated that five categories of KEGG orthology (COMT, HCT, CCR, CAD, and E1.14.11.61) involved in phenylpropanoid biosynthesis and six (DFR, CHS, LAR, CYP75A, FLS, and C12RT1) involved in flavonoid biosynthesis were significantly enriched in tandem duplicates. Structural genes involved in the two pathways with high expression in leaves generally played important roles in governing catechin contents, such as <i>CsF3H</i>, <i>CsANS</i>, and <i>CsF3</i>ʹ<i>5</i>ʹ<i>H</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Guo, F., Guo, Y., Wang, P., Wang, Y. &amp; Ni, D. Transcriptional profiling of catechins biosynthesis genes during tea plant leaf development. Planta 246, 1139–1152 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d4353e1105">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Jin, J. Q., Ma, J. Q., Yao, M. Z., Ma, C. L. &amp; Chen, L. Functional natural allelic variants of flavonoid 3’,5’-hydroxylase gene governing catechin traits in tea plant and its relatives. Planta 245, 523–538 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d4353e1108">46</a></sup>. Most highly expressed genes in the apical buds of these orthologs occurred in tandem duplication except for CAD, CCR, and FLS (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#Fig5">5d</a>). For example, six F3ʹ5ʹH genes belonging to CYP75A that could regulate catechin contents and the ratio of di/tri-hydroxylated catechins were identified in two clusters (chr11:134.5–135.1 Mb and chr7:43.7–43.8 Mb). Of these, the highly expressed <i>TEA013315.1</i> and <i>TEA026296.1</i> have been previously cloned as <i>CsF3</i>ʹ<i>5</i>ʹ<i>Ha</i> and <i>CsF3</i>ʹ<i>5</i>ʹ<i>Hb</i>, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Guo, L. et al. Functional analysis of flavonoid 3’-hydroxylase and flavonoid 3’,5’-hydroxylases from tea plant (Camellia sinensis), involved in the B-ring hydroxylation of flavonoids. Gene 717, 144046 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d4353e1141">47</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#Fig5">5e</a>). It has been demonstrated that <i>TEA013315.1</i> plays a crucial role in the concentration of catechins and could explain the variation in catechin contents among tea germplasms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Jin, J. Q., Ma, J. Q., Yao, M. Z., Ma, C. L. &amp; Chen, L. Functional natural allelic variants of flavonoid 3’,5’-hydroxylase gene governing catechin traits in tea plant and its relatives. Planta 245, 523–538 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d4353e1151">46</a></sup>. Tandem duplication increased functionally divergent genes that play important roles in tea-specific biosynthesis or stress response through sub- or neofunctionalization of the retained tandem duplicates, especially through evolution of gene expression.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Anchored QTL map</h3><p>Catechin and caffeine, major secondary metabolites in young leaves of tea, contribute to the tea flavor and nutrient content. Among the catechins in green tea, (−)-epigallocatechin-3-gallate (EGCG) is the most abundant, followed by (−)-epigallocatechin (EGC), (−)-epicatechin-3-gallate (ECG), and (−)-epicatechin (EC)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Balentine, D. A., Wiseman, S. A. &amp; Bouwens, L. C. The chemistry of tea flavonoids. Crit. Rev. Food Sci. Nutr. 37, 693–704 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d4353e1163">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Shahidi, F. Antioxidants in food and food antioxidants. Nahrung 44, 158–163 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d4353e1166">49</a></sup>. Based on previously reported QTLs related to the catechins and caffeine content in tea, 64 catechin- and caffeine-related QTLs were anchored to the CSS V1.2 genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#Fig6">6</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#MOESM2">8</a>). Among these, eight caffeine-related QTLs were distributed in chr1, chr2, chr3, chr10, and chr11. The poor reproducibility of the caffeine-related QTLs identified in different experiments might be caused by the different parents used for the mapping populations. The distribution of catechin-related QTLs indicated that the EC-, ECG-, EGC-, and EGCG-related QTLs were closely linked in some regions. Six catechin-related QTL hotspots were detected on chr3, chr6, chr7, chr10, chr11, and chr15. The QTL hotspots in chr7 and chr11 showed high phenotypic variance explained (PVE &gt; 20%). However, two QTL hotspots were not stable between the two populations of CSS ‘Yingshuang’ × <i>C. sinensis</i> var. <i>pubilimba</i> ‘Beiyue Danzhu’ and ‘TRFK 303/577’ × ‘GW Ejulu’. The analysis of WGD events implied that the regions of two hotspots were generated by the CRT event (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#Fig3">3</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#MOESM1">2</a>). These results revealed that the QTL hotspots in chr11 might play an essential role in catechin diversification of Chinese tea germplasms, but chr7 might be responsible for tea germplasms in South Africa. In the QTL hotspot of chr11, the F3ʹ5ʹH gene <i>TEA013315.1</i> has been cloned and validated to govern catechin traits in tea plant and its relatives. The tea genome sequence will provide the basis for the polymorphic markers, genes, and repeats within these QTL regions and facilitate the identification of their effective genes.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Distribution of QTLs associated with catechin and caffeine in the C. sinensis genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: Distribution of QTLs associated with catechin and caffeine in the <i>C. sinensis</i> genome<i>.</i></b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0288-2/MediaObjects/41438_2020_288_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0288-2/MediaObjects/41438_2020_288_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="191" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>CAF caffeine content, C catechin, EC epicatechin, ECG epicatechin gallate, EGC epigallocatechin, EGCG epigallocatechin gallate. The arrow represents QTLs with high phenotypic variance explained (PVE &gt; 20%)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>The first chromosome-scale genome of a highly heterozygous tea plant was successfully accomplished by Hi-C technology. The scaffold N50 of our assembly has been increased to 218.1 Mb, which is significantly higher than that of the draft genome. Moreover, collinearity comparison with the genetic map revealed that the chromosome-scale genome presented high contiguity and accuracy of assembly. Our results demonstrated that Hi-C technology could provide a rapid, suitable, and inexpensive approach to generate a high-quality chromosome-scale assembly compared with traditional approaches, such as BAC-by-BAC and genetic maps. These traditional approaches are confronted with many challenges when used for assembly of rather complex genomes (large genome size, polyploid, and high heterozygosity) because they are laborious and expensive. In contrast, Hi-C technology has overcome the existence insufficiency and become broadly available for many complex species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Zhang, J. et al. Allele-defined genome of the autopolyploid sugarcane Saccharum spontaneum L. Nat. Genet. 50, 1565–1573 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d4353e1215">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Bickhart, D. M. et al. Single-molecule sequencing and chromatin conformation capture enable de novo reference assembly of the domestic goat genome. Nat. Genet. 49, 643–650 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d4353e1218">51</a></sup>. Although Hi-C technology has accelerated the completion of chromosome-scale assembly of tea plants, our assembly is not perfect due to some misjoins of small adjacent contigs and missing bases. In the future, additional high-coverage PacBio long reads will be applied to correct the misjoins and fill the gaps.</p><p>Through the analysis of WGD events in the <i>C. sinensis</i> genome, we clarified that only one recent tetraploidization event (CRT, 58.9–61.7 Mya) occurred after the ancient hexaploidization event (ECH, 146.6–152.7 Mya) shared by the eudicot genome. A phylogenetic tree of homologous genes was constructed, and it was inferred that the CRT event occurred after the divergence of <i>C. sinensis</i> and <i>A. chinensis</i> from their common ancestor (61.2–65.3 Mya)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Xia, E. H. et al. The tea tree genome provides insights into tea flavor and independent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 10, 866–877 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4353e1234">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Wu, H. et al. A high-quality Actinidia chinensis (kiwifruit) genome. Horticulture Res. 6, 117 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d4353e1237">36</a></sup>. After polyploidization events, the genomic architecture was surprisingly varied, including massive genomic rearrangements, homologous exchange, gene loss of duplicates, proliferation of TEs, and gene expression bias of duplicates, which play an important role in the formation and evolution of species. Small RNAs, as mediators of interactions in duplicated genomes, have influences on genomic architecture that reflect their functional roles<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Wendel, J. F., Jackson, S. A., Meyers, B. C. &amp; Wing, R. A. Evolution of plant genome architecture. Genome Biol. 17, 37 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d4353e1241">31</a></sup>. Understanding the fundamental genomic structure accelerates plant improvement and utilization. For example, in maize, ~50% phenotype- or function-associated variation is more likely to have paralogs derived from the polyploidization event than singleton genes, suggesting that gene duplication followed by neofunctionalization or subfunctionalization plays an essential role in phenotypic variation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Wallace, J. G. et al. Association mapping across numerous traits reveals patterns of functional variation in maize. PLoS Genet. 10, e1004845 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d4353e1246">52</a></sup>. Our observation that extraordinary variation in genome size, gene loss, gene expression, and tandem duplicates burst after the CRT event. Two QTL hotspots in chr11 and chr7 derived from the CRT event might be responsible for catechin diversification of the tea germplasms.</p><p>Tea is one of the most popular nonalcoholic beverages in the world and has numerous health benefits for humans. The availability of a chromosome-scale genome holds great promise for the understanding of fundamental genomic architecture and evolution, and for the improvement of tea plant quality (such as catechin, theanine and caffeine) and yield. Integrated with GWAS, map-based clone, and bulked segregant analysis, the chromosome-scale genome will accelerate the identification of trait-related variations or genes that can be used for the rapid, effective, and inexpensive selection of available germplasms or cultivars.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Hi-C sequencing and genome sequence</h3><p>Fresh leaves of CSS ‘Shuchazao’ grown at the China National Germplasm Tea Repository (Hangzhou, Zhejiang, China) were selected for Hi-C sequencing. An in situ Hi-C library based on DpnII was constructed as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Rao, S. S. et al. A 3D map of the human genome at kilobase resolution reveals principles of chromatin looping. Cell 159, 1665–1680 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d4353e1265">53</a></sup> and the resulting library with an insert size of ~300 bp was sequenced on a HiSeq 4000 under 2 × 150 bp mode. The draft genome sequence of CSS ‘Shuchazao’ was downloaded from the Tea Plant Information Archive (TPIA, <a href="http://tpia.teaplant.org/">http://tpia.teaplant.org/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wei, C. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E4151–E4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d4353e1273">13</a></sup>. The gene annotation and TE annotation used in this study were also obtained from the draft genome sequence of CSS ‘Shuchazao’<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wei, C. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E4151–E4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d4353e1277">13</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec10">Chromosome assembly with Hi-C</h3><p>Raw Hi-C reads were mapped to the draft genome sequence using bwa (version 0.7.17-r1188)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d4353e1289">54</a></sup>, and then erroneous mappings (MAPQ = 0) and duplicates were filtered by the Juicer pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Durand, N. C. et al. Juicer provides a one-click system for analyzing loop-resolution Hi-C experiments. Cell Syst. 3, 95–98 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d4353e1293">55</a></sup>. The output of the Juicer pipeline was used for 3D-DNA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Dudchenko, O. et al. De novo assembly of the Aedes aegypti genome using Hi-C yields chromosome-length scaffolds. Science 356, 92–95 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0288-2#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d4353e1297">56</a></sup> analysis with default parameters, including misjoin correction, ordering, and orientation.
